  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_51ee9d76-0aee-4034-a086-c594e1aac9dd'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();

<span id="freeTextContainer10401715178744017730">“A superb mirror of a place, a time, and a group of people who capture our immediate interest and hold it tightly.” —The Philadelphia Inquirer<br/><br/>Young Reuven Malter is unsure of himself and his place in life.   <span id="freeText10401715178744017730" style="display:none">“A superb mirror of a place, a time, and a group of people who capture our immediate interest and hold it tightly.” —The Philadelphia Inquirer<br /><br />Young Reuven Malter is unsure of himself and his place in life. An unconventional scholar, he struggles for recognition from his teachers. With his old friend Danny Saunders—who himself had abandoned the legacy as the chosen heir to his father's rabbinical dynasty for the uncertain life of a healer—Reuvan battles to save a sensitive boy imprisoned by his genius and rage. Painfully, triumphantly, Reuven's understanding of himself, though the boy change, as he starts to approach the peace he has long sought…</span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I read the Promise as a standalone, for no other reason than I picked it up in my library and it looked interesting. I have not yet read the Chosen so<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I read the Promise as a standalone, for no other reason than I picked it up in my library and it looked interesting. I have not yet read the Chosen so I don't know if it will give me better insight into this novel once I do read it, but I do not feel like my understanding was impacted by having read the books out of order. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div id="review_1417550827" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1417550827" />
    <a title="Poiema" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1524767-poiema"><img alt="Poiema" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352414613p2/1524767.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1417550827?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Poiema" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Poiema" href="/user/show/1524767-poiema">Poiema</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1524767-poiema?shelf=jewish-interest">jewish-interest</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1417550827" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13254620652754080134"> I love, love, love Chaim Potok's writing. He has a depth that begs slow digestion and his ability to portray the evolution of cultural thought is pure genius. He uses Jewish culture as his backdrop, but the hardening of old positions vs. the embracing of new ideas is a theme that holds universal application. <br/><br/>This novel is a continuation of a previous novel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/187181.The_Chosen' title='The Chosen by Chaim Potok' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Chosen</a>. It is a story of the unlikely friendship between 2 young Jews from Brooklyn (1940s). Unlikely, because they are from two very d</span>
  <span id="freeText13254620652754080134" style="display:none"> I love, love, love Chaim Potok's writing. He has a depth that begs slow digestion and his ability to portray the evolution of cultural thought is pure genius. He uses Jewish culture as his backdrop, but the hardening of old positions vs. the embracing of new ideas is a theme that holds universal application. <br /><br />This novel is a continuation of a previous novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/187181.The_Chosen" title="The Chosen by Chaim Potok" rel="nofollow noopener">The Chosen</a>. It is a story of the unlikely friendship between 2 young Jews from Brooklyn (1940s). Unlikely, because they are from two very disparate sects of Judaism. There is unavoidable tension because they both hold tightly to their heritage, but their youth renders some elasticity. Ultimately their friendship serves as a bridge to bring some intercourse between the sects, some honor and respect and understanding. <br /><br />In this sequel, the young Danny has fulfilled his life goal of becoming a psychologist---a monumental decision because his destiny was preordained. He was slated to take his father's seat as the next Tzaddik (rabbinic leader) of the Hassidic sect into which he was born. To embrace psychology is to bring modern thought, something quite foreign to the ancient, unbending traditions. But Danny manages to hold to the traditions while at the same time expanding them; as a psychologist he becomes a "Tzaddik to the world." All of these expansions of mind, soul, and tradition are accomplished by the amalgamation that occurs between Danny and his more liberal Jewish friend, Reuven. The friendship is an incredible thing to witness through the pages of this book. <br /><br />I love the symbolism that Potok uses in his books: the Ailanthus tree, "the tree of heaven," is inserted in both books. The closed/open window is a symbol fitly woven into The Promise, portending the alternating stifling of new ideas and the opening of the mind.<br /><br />I'll be returning to this author; his writing provides riches for the heart and mind.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13254620652754080134" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_1557271891" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1557271891" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7022024-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470679918p2/7022024.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1557271891?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/7022024-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1557271891" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13361146095025108220"><i>(Some spoilers for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/187181.The_Chosen' title='The Chosen by Chaim Potok' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Chosen</a> follow)</i><br/><br/>Once again Potok delivers a very nuanced and human story that takes place during a time of flux for America and the Jewish community (though its message transcends sectarian bounds). This book is a continuation of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/187181.The_Chosen' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Chosen</a> and follows Reuven and Danny as they begin to enter their respective professional sphere: Danny in psychology and Reuven in Talmudic studies on the path to become a rabbi. They remain close, if busy, friends. America, however, is very dif</span>
  <span id="freeText13361146095025108220" style="display:none"><i>(Some spoilers for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/187181.The_Chosen" title="The Chosen by Chaim Potok" rel="nofollow noopener">The Chosen</a> follow)</i><br /><br />Once again Potok delivers a very nuanced and human story that takes place during a time of flux for America and the Jewish community (though its message transcends sectarian bounds). This book is a continuation of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/187181.The_Chosen" rel="nofollow noopener">The Chosen</a> and follows Reuven and Danny as they begin to enter their respective professional sphere: Danny in psychology and Reuven in Talmudic studies on the path to become a rabbi. They remain close, if busy, friends. America, however, is very different. American troops are fighting and dying in Korea instead of Europe, McCarthy is being a jackass, and recently immigrated European Jews, the survivors of the Holocaust, are beginning to change landscape of American Judaism.<br /><br />There are several drivers of conflict within this book, most of them revolving around Jewish identity. Reuven has been raised in an <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" rel="nofollow noopener">Orthodox</a> household, but a very modern one. His father is a world renowned Talmudic Scholar but utilizes scriptural analytical techniques that many recent Jewish immigrants find dangerous to their view of sacred texts. I won't bog you down with the minutia of it (which Potok does an excellent job explaining) but the technique assumes the people who wrote and transcribed them (not to mention interpreted them) were human and susceptible to making errors. A well read and knowledgeable modern reader with access to more documents can make educated guesses to clarify and reinterpret those texts. <br /><br />The recent immigrants from Europe who saw their culture, way of life, and loved ones go up in smoke during the war defend their view of scripture zealously, opposing the work of Reuven's father and similar scholars in public and private forums. One of the teachers Reuven must take class with and who will determine if he is allowed to become a rabbi is passionately opposed to this modern interpretation of scripture and leads the charge against Reuven's father. This friction between Reuven and his teacher is a source of anxiety stress for Reuven, making him consider a life in academia instead of the rabbinate. But there aren't really villains in this conflict, just well meaning people with different ideas about Judaism based on their life experiences. The recent immigrants saw so much of their past lives destroyed that they clung to their intepretation of scripture like a drowning man to flotsam.<i>
  <blockquote>"When your world is destroyed and only a remnant is saved, then whatever is seen as a threat to that remnant becomes a hated enemy."</blockquote>
</i> Potok does a wonderful job humanizing all his characters and imbuing them with wonderfully unique and realistic motivations. There were multiple plot lines going in the book that were weaved togethe rin a subtle, but profound, way. However, much like The Chosen, I felt that Reuven was the least interesting character of the lot. He was certainly more interesting and faced more challenges than in the previous book, but all the other characters had a lot more interesting things going on in their lives. Still, it was a wonderful and engaging read as well as a fitting conclusion to the story of Danny and Reuven.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13361146095025108220" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_10044315" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10044315" />
    <a title="Tora" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/635985-tora"><img alt="Tora" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452717479p2/635985.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10044315?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tora" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tora" href="/user/show/635985-tora">Tora</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/635985-tora?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/635985-tora?shelf=americana">americana</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10044315" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11160193259583728863">I thought The Chosen couldn't be beat, but this one did it. Chaim Potok draws you into the lives of the characters; Reuven's internal struggle to figure out just "what kind" of Jew he is while still remaining true to the faith he learned from his father, Danny's empathy with Michael's suffering and his desire to prove that choosing psychology was the right thing to do... but most of all it is tragic to see how much humans tear each other apart - in this book it's between Hasidic and other Orthod</span>
  <span id="freeText11160193259583728863" style="display:none">I thought The Chosen couldn't be beat, but this one did it. Chaim Potok draws you into the lives of the characters; Reuven's internal struggle to figure out just "what kind" of Jew he is while still remaining true to the faith he learned from his father, Danny's empathy with Michael's suffering and his desire to prove that choosing psychology was the right thing to do... but most of all it is tragic to see how much humans tear each other apart - in this book it's between Hasidic and other Orthodox Jews - but it reflects how those who are most similar to us are sometimes the easiest ones to hate and the hardest to love. But, Potok brilliantly brings the characters together in a way that gives hope for those, like Reuven, who seek to find a "middle" way that is still faithful. The title "The Promise" reflects the twofold nature of the people of Israel's faithfulness to God and YHWH's faithfulness to his people. <br /><br />Penetrating insight into Hasidism, New York in the 1950s and 60s, and a level of scholarship, academic discipline, and religious fervour that is becoming more and more foreign to our "modern" sensibilities. A simply brilliant book. I cannot wait to read Davita's Harp and finish the trilogy. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11160193259583728863" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_33153553" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33153553" />
    <a title="Reshma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56599-reshma"><img alt="Reshma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1176333000p2/56599.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33153553?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Reshma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Reshma" href="/user/show/56599-reshma">Reshma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33153553" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1067535162943709685">I read this book immediately after I read The Chosen. As a sequel, the reader expects from The Promise "more of the same" or even less. While the novel is not as deep thematically and symbolically as the first book, it maintains the warmth and genuineness of The Chosen. I read this book becasue I fell in love with the main characters, Danny and Reuven. The Promise is again written from the point of view of Reuven, but seems to talk less about Danny and more about the newly introduced characters.</span>
  <span id="freeText1067535162943709685" style="display:none">I read this book immediately after I read The Chosen. As a sequel, the reader expects from The Promise "more of the same" or even less. While the novel is not as deep thematically and symbolically as the first book, it maintains the warmth and genuineness of The Chosen. I read this book becasue I fell in love with the main characters, Danny and Reuven. The Promise is again written from the point of view of Reuven, but seems to talk less about Danny and more about the newly introduced characters. However, I felt that even though the text is less substantial in the amount of time spent on Danny, you get an equally in-depth insight into his life and experiences because of the awareness made of his character to the reader in the first book. In that regard, it satisfied my need to pursue these two characters. <br /><br />Both The Chosen and The Promise are not highly adventurous, exciting, tail spinning stories. In fact, they are just the opposite: calm, rational, seemingly simple relays of events and thoughts. Yet I couldn't put them down. There is something about the way Chaim Potok writes that not just makes you want to read his stories, but in fact respectfully welcomes you in to become intimate with the characters' thoughts and feelings. It is not that other books have made me feel like an intruder; but, simply that these two books have made me feel openly welcomed.<br /><br />It's a great story about life and personal growth. I recommend it to any reader who likes to think introspectively when they read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1067535162943709685" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3767809-trace?shelf=2013-booklist">2013-booklist</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer478410057" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15072395650898759794">I'm trying to be more selective when dishing out 5 star ratings... really, I am. But this book truly deserves every one of these 5 stars!<br/>And I'm going to be hard pressed to explain exactly WHY! Chaim Potok is such a brilliant author and his writing is SO very elegant and layered but try as I might, I cannot put a finger on PRECISELY why his books are so special... <br/><br/>It deals with some topics I find distasteful (I do not like what happened to poor Michael for instance) and I WANTED to deduct stars </span>
  <span id="freeText15072395650898759794" style="display:none">I'm trying to be more selective when dishing out 5 star ratings... really, I am. But this book truly deserves every one of these 5 stars!<br />And I'm going to be hard pressed to explain exactly WHY! Chaim Potok is such a brilliant author and his writing is SO very elegant and layered but try as I might, I cannot put a finger on PRECISELY why his books are so special... <br /><br />It deals with some topics I find distasteful (I do not like what happened to poor Michael for instance) and I WANTED to deduct stars for that reason... but found I could not.... <br /><br />I LOVE that this book, just like its prequel The Chosen (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/249830307">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a>), left me with so many questions....I find the truly great books tend to do this....<br /><br />Also there were MANY themes intertwined in the Promise... enough that I'm sure this book will still seem fresh even on a 6th reading.... <br />One theme in this book that I find fascinating is the pull and tug between the traditional and the modern...<br /><br />Another thing that fascinated me is the way Potok would sprinkle references to James Joyce's Ulysses throughout the story... Ulysses has been on my to-read list for years and I still don't feel quite brave enough to tackle it - but Potok has me VERY intrigued with his references to the Ithaca and Penelope chapters...!!<br /><br />And of course I adore the fact that the characters in the two Potok books that I've read so far have a hunger for studying and learning... <br /><br />I'm glad I own a copy of this book - I know I'll want to reread it again and again. But first I need to let it marinade a bit more...<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15072395650898759794" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4176602-shaimaa-ali?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer473749900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3821736397585603665">I wanted to write a long review on this marvelous novel ..However I think I won't find enough words to describe my admiration!<br/><br/>This is the sequel of Potok's novel "The Chosen" Which I didn't think anything can beat it , We have the same old characters beside lots of other new scholars and their families. <br/>Potok continues drawing his characters that you feel you already can see & feel them , his major strength point in writing is the dialogue between characters and the description of places & peopl</span>
  <span id="freeText3821736397585603665" style="display:none">I wanted to write a long review on this marvelous novel ..However I think I won't find enough words to describe my admiration!<br /><br />This is the sequel of Potok's novel "The Chosen" Which I didn't think anything can beat it , We have the same old characters beside lots of other new scholars and their families. <br />Potok continues drawing his characters that you feel you already can see &amp; feel them , his major strength point in writing is the dialogue between characters and the description of places &amp; people that is neither lengthy nor too short.. His weak point is the description of discussions without focusing on a single topic or giving an example .. maybe I wanted that cos I liked to focus on how do Jews study their religious books, are they complex? how they find contradictions &amp; assertions in other places? and so many real questions not imaginary situations.<br /><br />Our old characters (Danny &amp; Reuven) have been matured now, one can't do anything but fall in love with Danny's character, while Reuven drove me crazy with his silence in so many important situations .." he just didn't find anything to say" quoting the writer in so many places.. <br /><br />I was amazed by the similarities between Hasidics and Radical Muslims, they get married to virgins, the separation between males &amp; females while worshiping and even in weddings!! This came to me as a shock ..so we "Muslims" are not the only reserved people in the world! and religious people in all around the world had many things to share!! Even the strange feelings that Reuven , Rachel &amp; her family felt while dealing with Danny's family was a great deal to consider!<br /><br />During the middle chapters and the confrontations/contradictions between East Europe method of teaching, Pink Floyd's The Wall-Part2 was running in my mind in the background : "We don't need no education ..We don't need no thought control" !! <br /><br />Potok simply reserve Noble Prize, I wonder why he didn't get it till now!<br /><br />P.S: Again I'm here evaluating the literature and the great gift the writer has as you can imagine how different his thoughts can be from an Arab Muslim girl! <br /> </span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12534928798437476518"> <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1071974.The_Promise' title='The Promise by Chaim Potok' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Promise</a> is the continuation of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7385.Chaim_Potok' title='Chaim Potok' rel='nofollow noopener'>Chaim Potok</a>'s brilliant novel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/607274.The_Chosen' title='The Chosen by Chaim Potok' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Chosen</a> which I loved. But I was slightly disappointed with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1071974.The_Promise' title='The Promise by Chaim Potok' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Promise</a>. It did not capture my heart as did <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/607274.The_Chosen' title='The Chosen by Chaim Potok' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Chosen</a>. <br/> <br/>Don't get me wrong, I liked <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1071974.The_Promise' title='The Promise by Chaim Potok' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Promise</a>. But...<br/> <br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1071974.The_Promise' title='The Promise by Chaim Potok' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Promise</a> finds Danny Saunders and Reuven Malters finishing up their studies.   <span id="freeText12534928798437476518" style="display:none"> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1071974.The_Promise" title="The Promise by Chaim Potok" rel="nofollow noopener">The Promise</a> is the continuation of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7385.Chaim_Potok" title="Chaim Potok" rel="nofollow noopener">Chaim Potok</a>'s brilliant novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/607274.The_Chosen" title="The Chosen by Chaim Potok" rel="nofollow noopener">The Chosen</a> which I loved. But I was slightly disappointed with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1071974.The_Promise" title="The Promise by Chaim Potok" rel="nofollow noopener">The Promise</a>. It did not capture my heart as did <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/607274.The_Chosen" title="The Chosen by Chaim Potok" rel="nofollow noopener">The Chosen</a>. <br /> <br />Don't get me wrong, I liked <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1071974.The_Promise" title="The Promise by Chaim Potok" rel="nofollow noopener">The Promise</a>. A promise to one of Reuven's Talmud instructors and one to a very sick boy filled with rage will have have both young men questioning their judgement and test their capacity for forgiveness. <br /> <br />I can't go into the plot because I will reveal spoilers. Potok crafts his story lines well and again his characters (most of them) are warm and multidimensional. There is the conflict between tradition and the new ideas. There is a boy not yet 13 whom is full of rage and sickness. There are a few other subtle complex story lines that Potok deals with beautifully. </span>

    <a title="Karl Zimmerman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/67562555-karl-zimmerman"><img alt="Karl Zimmerman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588530487p2/67562555.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1999183415" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9746755245714001025">Potok goes digging about in such messy but realistic parts of life. I think that's why I care about his books. <br/>His books aren't very full of action and can feel slightly dull, but the reader is rewarded with moments of both vivid beauty and pain. <br/><br/>While reading The Promise, I appreciated (and was thrown off balance by) how nuanced Potok's characterizations are. As a result, I sympathized with both sides of the conflict. He didn't whitewash or vilify. There were things to love and things to detest</span>
  <span id="freeText9746755245714001025" style="display:none">Potok goes digging about in such messy but realistic parts of life. I think that's why I care about his books. <br />His books aren't very full of action and can feel slightly dull, but the reader is rewarded with moments of both vivid beauty and pain. <br /><br />While reading The Promise, I appreciated (and was thrown off balance by) how nuanced Potok's characterizations are. As a result, I sympathized with both sides of the conflict. He didn't whitewash or vilify. There were things to love and things to detest in the lives of almost all of the characters--just as in the complicated world I live in. <br /><br />In this story, he paints so compellingly the tension between religious zealotry and enlightened skepticism (and the accompanying dust cloud of misunderstandings and suspicions). <br />The Jews are not the only people to face this. I do too. </span>

<div id="review_810012671" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/810012671" />
    <a title="Amy Edwards" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19020907-amy-edwards"><img alt="Amy Edwards" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509302749p2/19020907.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/810012671?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy Edwards" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy Edwards" href="/user/show/19020907-amy-edwards">Amy Edwards</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer810012671" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16408147619090236055">In The Promise, a follow-up to The Chosen, we catch up with Reuven Malter as he is continuing his graduate education in the 1950s Jewish community of New York. While The Chosen focused on Reuven's life-altering friendship with Danny as the two boys found their way to manhood, The Promise deals with the clash of belief and unbelief, tradition and secularism, Orthodoxy and unorthodoxy, and supernaturalism and naturalism that hit the post-war American Jewish community. Secularism was a rising force</span>
  <span id="freeText16408147619090236055" style="display:none">In The Promise, a follow-up to The Chosen, we catch up with Reuven Malter as he is continuing his graduate education in the 1950s Jewish community of New York. While The Chosen focused on Reuven's life-altering friendship with Danny as the two boys found their way to manhood, The Promise deals with the clash of belief and unbelief, tradition and secularism, Orthodoxy and unorthodoxy, and supernaturalism and naturalism that hit the post-war American Jewish community. Secularism was a rising force before the war, but the horrors of the concentration camps and the anti-Semitism of Germany had shaken Judaism and Jewish scholars and Rabbis reacted differently. Some stood firm in the belief in a God who verbally and miraculously gave the Torah to Moses and in The Promise. This is represented by Rav Kalman, Reuven's teacher. Rav Kalman stands as a fierce fighter for the old ways and the old belief, even as more "enlightened" Jews turn away. Other Jews sought to find a way forward in the world of unbelief while still maintaining their identity and culture as Jews, and The Promise's Abraham Gordon represents this extreme. Reuven stands between the two clashing ideas, and his own father provides a middle way, of sorts. <br /><br />Reuven finds himself drawn into a close relationship with the Gordon family and as the book opens we find that he is dating Gordon's niece and befriending Gordon's troubled son. Before long, the Gordon family seeks Danny's clinical psychology services for their son, and the plot pulls together characters whose ideas repel them from one another.<br /><br />At one point in the book, Rav Kalman asks Reuven accusingly about his association with the secularist Abraham Gordon. As Reuven's smicha hangs in the balance, Kalman wants to know how Gordon's writings have influenced Reuven. "No," Reuven replies, "I don't like his answers." What about his questions, asks Rav Kalman. "I ask the same questions," Reuven responds. But Reuven has yet to find the answers.<br /><br />This is the crux of the book. For Judaism in particular, the events of the holocaust brought a crisis of faith. Where was the promised Messiah? How could this happen to God's chosen people? His treasured possession? First the pogroms in Russia, then the Nazi concentration camps. Where was God? With the forces of naturalism already sweeping through intellectualism, Orthodox belief was in peril. But even for Gentiles, the central question of the book is a question for all of us. Is God real? Must we abandon belief? Does modernity make faith obsolete?<br /><br />As a Christian, I believe that the answer to Reuven's questions is found in Jesus. The Messiah came, and the promise was kept. God is real and His reality demands a response. <br /><br />While I loved The Chosen and would recommend it to my teenagers, I think that The Promise is more difficult to understand without having a context in place historically and spiritually. I am sure a young reader would enjoy the plot of the book and enjoy discovering what happens to Danny and Reuven, but I would suggest that The Promise is a book best read after already gaining a strong understanding of 20th century history and philosophy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16408147619090236055" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6435025200579526813">upon rereading (the chosen) for about the third time and reading the sequel (the promise) for the first time, i think chaim potok is now one of my favorite authors. a great storyteller. with seemingly simple sentences and straightforward descriptions, draws us into reuven and danny's world: a time and place and religion: brooklyn, world war i and ii, orthodox judaism. books that many may not pick up for the synopsis alone, but the stories resonate because they are really about friendship (how di</span>
  <span id="freeText6435025200579526813" style="display:none">upon rereading (the chosen) for about the third time and reading the sequel (the promise) for the first time, i think chaim potok is now one of my favorite authors. a great storyteller. with seemingly simple sentences and straightforward descriptions, draws us into reuven and danny's world: a time and place and religion: brooklyn, world war i and ii, orthodox judaism. books that many may not pick up for the synopsis alone, but the stories resonate because they are really about friendship (how difficult it is to be a good one), choice/obligation/freedom, family. And the promise becomes much larger and is about revolution and changing the system in which you grew up and/or one you are trying to move into. figuring out how to fight those who don't want to change with respect and courage. dealing with hating and loving your family at the same time.</span>

<div id="review_2005726729" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2005726729" />
    <a title="Elizabeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9681387-elizabeth"><img alt="Elizabeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598585352p2/9681387.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2005726729?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth" href="/user/show/9681387-elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2005726729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11365385452998103332">The Chosen is probably one of my all time favorite books. My mom made me read it in high school (homeschooler here), I read it again for an American Lit class in college and a third time for a book club. Not many sequels are on par with the original book but this one is. Potok writes with such simplicity, such insight. Even if you know nothing about Hasidic or Orthodox Judaism or the conflicts regarding the various traditions (which I didn’t), it just sucks you in. I loved seeing how the charact</span>
  <span id="freeText11365385452998103332" style="display:none">The Chosen is probably one of my all time favorite books. My mom made me read it in high school (homeschooler here), I read it again for an American Lit class in college and a third time for a book club. Not many sequels are on par with the original book but this one is. Potok writes with such simplicity, such insight. Even if you know nothing about Hasidic or Orthodox Judaism or the conflicts regarding the various traditions (which I didn’t), it just sucks you in. I loved seeing how the characters from The Chosen continued to grow and struggle and think through what they believed. This book made me think about what a culture or faith group does in response to a shifting belief system when they have come out of a period of persecution (this book highlights the struggles of European Jews emigrating to the US after being interned in concentration camps). When you feel like the beliefs and lifestyle you suffered for are even vaguely threatened and your suffering therefore seems meaningless, what do you do? How do you respect others who think differently, how do you love them, how do you confront what you believe are truly essential threats, how do you even sort out what are real or simply perceived threats? Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11365385452998103332" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_265797340" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/265797340" />
    <a title="Flavia Gaia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7452971-flavia-gaia"><img alt="Flavia Gaia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1327032104p2/7452971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/265797340?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Flavia Gaia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Flavia Gaia" href="/user/show/7452971-flavia-gaia">Flavia Gaia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer265797340" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11587538411242220239">The Promise is a novel written by Chaim Potok and published as a sequel to The Chosen, published two years earlier. It was originally published by Alfred A. Knopf in New York. Although not quite as impressive as The Chosen, The Promise is still laudable, skillfully woven to a depth which many modern books tend to lack.<br/>The Chosen takes place in Brooklyn, New York, amongst the various Jewish sects which resided there in the 1940’s. The book begins with Reuven Malter, a Jewish student and son of pr</span>
  <span id="freeText11587538411242220239" style="display:none">The Promise is a novel written by Chaim Potok and published as a sequel to The Chosen, published two years earlier. It was originally published by Alfred A. Knopf in New York. Although not quite as impressive as The Chosen, The Promise is still laudable, skillfully woven to a depth which many modern books tend to lack.<br />The Chosen takes place in Brooklyn, New York, amongst the various Jewish sects which resided there in the 1940’s. The book begins with Reuven Malter, a Jewish student and son of prominent Talmud commentator David Malter, as 15 years old. For his entire life he has lived among the Hasidim, an extremely orthodox sect, and not known Danny Saunders, who lives 5 blocks away, and is the genius son of a Hasidic rabbi, Reb Saunders. Playing against each other in a baseball game, they meet and become friends, encouraged by David Malter. They grow together and Reuven learns of Danny’s strong wish to pass the role of rabbi onto his sickly younger brother and become a psychologist, while Reuven works toward becoming a rabbi himself. Various conflicts among Judaism, World War II, Hitler, and the Holocaust affect their lives. Danny’s religion and his father also take a toll on Reuven’s life, as he finds a deeply rooted struggle between father and son.<br />In The Promise, Reuven and Danny are both studying, Reuven for his smicha and a degree in philosophy, and Danny for a degree in psychology. They come into contact with a mentally sick boy, Michael. Michael is the son of Abraham Gordon, whose writings have caused much controversy among Jewish Talmudists. <br />Whereas the Torah is a book of law and prophecy given to Moses by God Himself, the Talmud contains the various interpretations of the Torah by ancient scholars. Therefore while it is a sacred book, it contradicts itself and is subject to emendation, and is constantly causing controversy among the modern Talmudists. <br />Reuven must work toward his smicha under a strict Rav Kalman (‘Rav’ being a title for those who have earned smicha, and are qualified to teach Talmud), who is extremely orthodox and feels that it is as much his duty to guard “the spark” as it is the Hasid’s. ‘The spark’ represents something along the lines of Yiddishkeit, or Judaism, and the ancient teachings and privilege to teach. He is against both Reuven’s father and Abraham Gordon, and Reuven still must work to earn smicha beneath him. During this, Danny is placed in charge of the treatment of Michael, and resorts to a desperate experiment in order to coerce Michael to talk in therapy. <br />The writing style is not overly descriptive, but simply tells the truth, which is what makes The Promise so successful. Certain scenes are so utterly realistic the reader is left with a hurting heart for the pain or rejoicing for the success of the characters. Also, Potok seemed to concentrate more on what happens around Reuven (as the story is told from Reuven’s point of view in first person) rather than what Reuven himself is doing; unlike some books, which are constantly sentences beginning with “I” (“I could smell this…” or “I walked there…”) This made the book not so simplistic to read, and also not so repetitive. It was interesting and very realistic. <br />While there wasn’t a lot of description per say, the descriptions were extremely realistic and artful. Again, they did not try to harangue the reader, but simply told the truth, which is what captures the reader’s attention. <br />Symbolism was prominent in this book, especially in nature. Various symbols were clouds, trees, stars, colors, and each played a secretive role in the lives of the characters. The reader is forced to question, and thus gains wisdom from the book. <br />The Jewish lives are portrayed as wonderful and challenging, and the dedication to religion astonishing to the laymen. Not only does the reader gain wisdom from the various themes, he/she also develops an appreciation for the rich religion and its ardent followers, and their mastery and dedication to scholarship. While entertaining, this book is also enlightening. <br />Chaim Potok’s The Promise masterfully illustrates the passion of Jewish scholars in America. In a seemingly simple way, it bares the truth, while intertwining classic themes and archetypes that each reader can interpret differently. Potok’s writing breaks away from the monotonous and overly descriptive pieces so highly praised today, giving the reader a new viewpoint many authors today are unable to achieve. A modern classic, The Promise will leave readers both enlightened at the wisdom and breathless at the sincerity of this piece.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11587538411242220239" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11783340685787015798">Another tremendous book by Chaim Potok. It is a continuation of the story in <i>The Chosen</i>. It was thought provoking in terms of presenting a number of challenging moral dilemmas, and I was forced to ask myself how I would deal with each scenario as it arose. The writing style is very appealing. The long carnival account in the opening of the book could have easily become boring if written by a less talented writer, but it was written in such a way that I wouldn't even think of putting the book dow</span>
  <span id="freeText11783340685787015798" style="display:none">Another tremendous book by Chaim Potok. It is a continuation of the story in <i>The Chosen</i>. It was thought provoking in terms of presenting a number of challenging moral dilemmas, and I was forced to ask myself how I would deal with each scenario as it arose. The writing style is very appealing. The long carnival account in the opening of the book could have easily become boring if written by a less talented writer, but it was written in such a way that I wouldn't even think of putting the book down until it was resolved. It dealt fairly with the struggle between religious traditionalism and modernism. <br /><br />I was hoping there might be a third book in this serious, but it appears that is not the case. That saddens me.</span>

<div id="review_1611095079" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1611095079" />
    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7240015-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485740487p2/7240015.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1611095079?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/7240015-stephen">Stephen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7240015-stephen?shelf=judaism">judaism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7240015-stephen?shelf=1960s">1960s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7240015-stephen?shelf=of-boys-and-men">of-boys-and-men</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7240015-stephen?shelf=fathers-and-sons">fathers-and-sons</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1611095079" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9512699484150344636">Growing up is never easy, but for Orthodox boys in the mid-20th century, it's especially hard. The Jewish people are in turmoil after the horrors of the Holocaust, some pinning their hopes on Israel and others recoiling from it as anathema. The latter is true of Hasidic communities from Eastern Europe, fleeing both European and Soviet persecution, finding safe haven in the United States. The welcome American Jews might have given to their kin, however, is worn thin by the Hasids' swelling number</span>
  <span id="freeText9512699484150344636" style="display:none">Growing up is never easy, but for Orthodox boys in the mid-20th century, it's especially hard. The Jewish people are in turmoil after the horrors of the Holocaust, some pinning their hopes on Israel and others recoiling from it as anathema. The latter is true of Hasidic communities from Eastern Europe, fleeing both European and Soviet persecution, finding safe haven in the United States. The welcome American Jews might have given to their kin, however, is worn thin by the Hasids' swelling number and their fervent defense of rigid Orthodoxy. In this setting Danny Saunders and Reuven Malter, two Orthodox boys introduced in the gripping tale of The Chosen, complete their coming of age, united in the treatment of a young boy whose genius is matched by his inexplicable rage.<br /><br />In The Chosen, Danny chose to depart from his father's legacy as a Hasidic rabbi, a leader of his community. He chose instead to pursue psychology, while his more mainstream rival-turned-friend Reuven realized a call to the rabbinate. The Promise opens with both young men engaged in their graduate studies, and both faced with shared difficulties that force them to reconsider the paths they have taken. The first challenge is a boy with a passion for astronomy, the son of a humanistic Jewish scholar who is the object of scorn to the traditionalists governing Hirsch University. Michael is very sick, possessed by fantasies and given to episodes of rage; he exhausts therapists and seemed doomed to be institutionalized. Both Danny and Reuven have a personal connection to the stargazer Michael, in being companions of his older cousin Rachel. Danny has an idea for how to treat Michael, but it's risky: if it fails, it may destroy the boy's psyche altogether. Meanwhile, Reuven's position as a graduate student who must soon defend his grasp and attitude of Talmud study to a panel of elders forces him between more liberal scholars like his father and Michael's, and the traditionalist Hasids. He recoils against the 'mental ghetto' of fundamentalist Talmud studies, but is not satisfied with answers that reduce Judaism to empty family traditions.<br /><br />In The Chosen, Potok impressed me by having Danny and Reuven both embroiled in an intense and challenging relationship with Danny's father, Reb Saunders, who despaired both of Danny's interest in the outside world, and of Reuven's own father's modernist approach to Talmudic study. Although they began as antagonists, however, ultimately they arrived at mutual understanding. No one is defeated, their differences do not cease, but they break through the arguments to re-embrace the people making them. Potok accomplishes something very like that here, in the person of Rev Kalman. Kalman survived the death camps of the Nazi state, but lost nearly everyone he knew, and when confronted with American Jews he sees challengers that threaten to complete by sophistry what Hitler began with direct industrial murder. Kalman stands between Reuven and ordination, and is an especially difficult antagonist given that he rails against Reuven's father in the press. Yet Potok does not resolve the tension by having Reuven choose a prescripted side. Instead, he makes his own choice, and Kalman proves to be much like Reb Saunders: the enmity is defeated, but not his person.<br /><br />Though initially appealing for being the further story of Danny and Reuven, Potok's skill at rendering intense debate that results in mutual understanding rather than one-sided triumphs impressed me. I imagine as a rabbi himself, Potok has spent long hours having similar heated conversations with his colleagues and academics, attempting to reconcile an ancient faith with modernity without losing the power of those values and practices to endue lives with direction and meaning.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9512699484150344636" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2918504959940530347">This spring, I applied to a college where I planned to major in Talmud, got in, and legitimately almost went despite not having studied Torah since my bat mitzvah and not being entirely sure what the Talmud is. This book is that good. <br/><br/>Additionally, as much as Reuven's Jewish world is different from my own, it is so good to see Jewish characters and Jewish stories that don't revolve entirely around the Holocaust, even though it does explore its impact.<br/><br/>I tend to be a skim-reader, but I always make</span>
  <span id="freeText2918504959940530347" style="display:none">This spring, I applied to a college where I planned to major in Talmud, got in, and legitimately almost went despite not having studied Torah since my bat mitzvah and not being entirely sure what the Talmud is. This book is that good. <br /><br />Additionally, as much as Reuven's Jewish world is different from my own, it is so good to see Jewish characters and Jewish stories that don't revolve entirely around the Holocaust, even though it does explore its impact.<br /><br />I tend to be a skim-reader, but I always make sure to slow down when I read these books, because every word Potok writes is worth reading.</span>

<div id="review_2036356219" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2036356219" />
    <a title="Laura (Book Scrounger)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33792597-laura-book-scrounger"><img alt="Laura (Book Scrounger)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541274882p2/33792597.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2036356219?book_show_action=true">Jun 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura (Book Scrounger)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura (Book Scrounger)" href="/user/show/33792597-laura-book-scrounger">Laura (Book Scrounger)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780449209103" href="/book/show/77466.The_Promise">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33792597-laura-book-scrounger?shelf=new-york-city">new-york-city</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33792597-laura-book-scrounger?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33792597-laura-book-scrounger?shelf=1960s">1960s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2036356219" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6583376633378785600">This story picks up a few years after <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/187181.The_Chosen' title='The Chosen by Chaim Potok' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Chosen</a> leaves off, with Reuven and Danny attending separate universities. It explores the tensions between fundamentalist and liberal elements of religion, and also explores in more depth the parent/child relationship, especially for those, such as Reuven's father, who are vocal and occasionally the target of harsh criticisms. <br/><br/>In this book, the fundamentalist aspect of Jewish faith is represented by Rav Kalman, one of Reuven's teachers, who disapproves o</span>
  <span id="freeText6583376633378785600" style="display:none">This story picks up a few years after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/187181.The_Chosen" title="The Chosen by Chaim Potok" rel="nofollow noopener">The Chosen</a> leaves off, with Reuven and Danny attending separate universities. It explores the tensions between fundamentalist and liberal elements of religion, and also explores in more depth the parent/child relationship, especially for those, such as Reuven's father, who are vocal and occasionally the target of harsh criticisms. <br /><br />In this book, the fundamentalist aspect of Jewish faith is represented by Rav Kalman, one of Reuven's teachers, who disapproves of the methods Reuven and his father use to study Talmud. On the other hand, Reuven begins an acquaintance with Abraham Gordon, a writer who is trying to bridge the gap between his atheism and his traditional Jewish roots. It seems that Reuven himself is somewhere in the middle, and these elements of the story are brought together in Michael, the mentally ill son of Abraham Gordon, who is being treated by Danny. <br /><br />As in the previous book, I liked the relationship between Reuven and his father. We don't see as much of Reuven and Danny together here, but their friendship and perspectives are still there. I really appreciated the complex and compassionate treatment of the "battle" between generations and also between conservative vs. liberal, which could have applications to other religion as well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6583376633378785600" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('s2Pvszvy4ahvxlAQdd5NhE03XtlOoS3sH/vbCXcGnr4p1NzRO25nmAbBaw/ge677Fk9gEAhbE9ESm/BR+5OvKQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kzm2uUqLAUpTEnVvAPoqde8vX5N5Q7aDzaWHQBDL1AQJjoXbSheHejoVTnCVX8kKtFdhWj+5iL7AxawYnF7lkw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Nadat het verhaal van zijn alternatieve therapie door een bekende auteur te boek is gesteld en een bestseller is geworden, raakt hij verstrikt in een web van halve waarheden<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText9044279192344831035\" style=\"display:none\">Otto Kadoke, de grensoverschrijdende psychiater uit Moedervlekken, is terug. Nadat het verhaal van zijn alternatieve therapie door een bekende auteur te boek is gesteld en een bestseller is geworden, raakt hij verstrikt in een web van halve waarheden, interpretaties en leugens dat hem zijn baan kost - en zijn reputatie vernietigt. Maar niet alleen dat: zijn moeder is inmiddels weer vader geworden en de verzorgster van vader ontpopt zich tot een onvermoeibare aanhangster van Christenen voor Israël.\nOp een dag, als alles verloren lijkt, staat een ver familielid voor de deur: in haar ziet Kadoke zijn kans om te ontsnappen. Ze voert hem - en zijn vader - naar de bezette gebieden, waar hij tegen zijn zin in als verlosser wordt onthaald.\nBezette gebieden is een hartverscheurende, maar ook zeer komische roman over het verleden dat toekomst en heden overwoekert, over schuldige affaires op onschuldige plekken, en onschuldige affaires op schuldige plekken.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"9044279192344831035\" href=\"#\" The significance of Scripture may not be immediate<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4360934179328933250\" style=\"display:none\">Today the Quran is used by some to justify war and acts of terrorism, the Torah to deny Palestinians the right to live in the Land of Israel, and the Bible to condemn homosexuality and contraception. The significance of Scripture may not be immediately obvious in our secular world, but its misunderstanding is perhaps the root cause of many of today\'s controversies.\n\nIn this timely and important book, one of the most trusted and admired writers on the world of faith examines the meaning of Scripture. The sacred texts have been coopted by fundamentalists, who insist that they must be taken literally, and by others who interpret Scripture to bolster their own prejudices. These texts are seen to prescribe ethical norms and codes of behavior that are divinely ordained: they are believed to contain eternal truths. But as Karen Armstrong shows in this chronicle of the development and significance of major religions, such a narrow, peculiar reading of Scripture is a relatively recent, modern phenomenon. For most of their history, the world\'s religious traditions have regarded these texts as tools that enable the individual to connect with the divine, to experience a different level of consciousness, and to help them engage with the world in more meaningful and compassionate ways.\n\nAt a time of intolerance and mutual incomprehension, The Lost Art of Scripture shines fresh light on the world\'s major religions to help us build bridges between faiths and rediscover a creative and spiritual engagement with holy texts.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"4360934179328933250\" href=\"#\" <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10274227326474647724">Herman Harold Potok, or Chaim Tzvi, was born in Buffalo, New York, to Polish immigrants. He received an Orthodox Jewish education. After reading Evelyn Waugh's novel Brideshead Revisited as a teenager, he decided to become a writer. He started writing fiction at the age of 16. At age 17 he made his first submission to the magazine The Atlantic Monthly. Although it wasn't published, he received a n</span>
  <span id="freeText10274227326474647724" style="display:none">Herman Harold Potok, or Chaim Tzvi, was born in Buffalo, New York, to Polish immigrants. He received an Orthodox Jewish education. After reading Evelyn Waugh's novel Brideshead Revisited as a teenager, he decided to become a writer. He started writing fiction at the age of 16. At age 17 he made his first submission to the magazine The Atlantic Monthly. Although it wasn't published, he received a note from the editor complimenting his work.<br /><br />In 1949, at the age of 20, his stories were published in the literary magazine of Yeshiva University, which he also helped edit. In 1950, Potok graduated summa cum laude with a BA in English Literature.<br /><br />After four years of study at the Jewish Theological Seminary of America he was ordained as a Conservative rabbi. He was appointed director of Leaders Training Fellowship, a youth organization affiliated with Conservative Judaism.<br /><br />After receiving a master's degree in English literature, Potok enlisted with the U.S. Army as a chaplain. He served in South Korea from 1955 to 1957. He described his time in S. Korea as a transformative experience. Brought up to believe that the Jewish people were central to history and God's plans, he experienced a region where there were almost no Jews and no anti-Semitism, yet whose religious believers prayed with the same fervor that he saw in Orthodox synagogues at home.<br /><br />Upon his return, he joined the faculty of the University of Judaism in Los Angeles and became the director of a Conservative Jewish summer camp affiliated with the Conservative movement, Camp Ramah. A year later he began his graduate studies at the University of Pennsylvania and was appointed scholar-in-residence at Temple Har Zion in Philadelphia.<br /><br />In 1963, he spent a year in Israel, where he wrote his doctoral dissertation on Solomon Maimon and began to write a novel.<br /><br />In 1964 Potok moved to Brooklyn. He became the managing editor of the magazine <em>Conservative Judaism</em> and joined the faculty of the Teachers’ Institute of the Jewish Theological Seminary. The following year, he was appointed editor-in-chief of the Jewish Publication Society in Philadelphia and later, chairman of the publication committee. Potok received a doctorate in philosophy from the University of Pennsylvania.<br /><br />In 1970, Potok relocated to Jerusalem with his family. He returned to Philadelphia in 1977. After the publication of <em>Old Men at Midnight</em>, he was diagnosed with brain cancer. He died at his home in Merion, Pennsylvania on July 23, 2002, aged 73.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10274227326474647724" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
